      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       This article is about Scandinavia as a cultural-linguistic region. For the broader group of northern European countries including Finland and Iceland, see
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">
        Nordic countries
       </a>
       . For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Scandinavia_(disambiguation)" title="Scandinavia (disambiguation)">
        Scandinavia (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>

      <p>
       <b>
        Scandinavia
       </b>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [a]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">
            ˌ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
            æ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">
            eɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'v' in 'vie'">
            v
           </span>
           <span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">
            i
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       is a region in
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">
        Northern Europe
       </a>
       , characterized by common ethnocultural
       <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">
        North Germanic heritage
       </a>
       and mutually intelligible
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North Germanic languages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The term
       <i>
        Scandinavia
       </i>
       in local usage covers the three
       <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy#Modern_constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">
        kingdoms
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">
        Denmark
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">
        Norway
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">
        Sweden
       </a>
       , but in English usage, it also sometimes refers to the
       <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Peninsula" title="Scandinavian Peninsula">
        Scandinavian Peninsula
       </a>
       or to the broader region which includes
       <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">
        Finland
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">
        Iceland
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEDdef_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-OEDdef-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This broader region is usually known locally as the
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">
        Nordic countries
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nordic_Council_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nordic_Council-4">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The remote Norwegian islands of
       <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">
        Svalbard
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen" title="Jan Mayen">
        Jan Mayen
       </a>
       are usually not seen as a part of Scandinavia, nor is
       <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">
        Greenland
       </a>
       , a constituent country within the Kingdom of Denmark. The
       <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">
        Faroe Islands
       </a>
       may be included.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-eb-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Toponymy">
        Toponymy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Toponymy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The name Scandinavia originally referred to the former Danish, now Swedish, region of
       <a href="/wiki/Scania" title="Scania">
        Scania
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        Scandinavia
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Scandinavian
       </i>
       entered usage in the late 18th century, being introduced by the early linguistic and cultural
       <a href="/wiki/Scandinavism" title="Scandinavism">
        Scandinavist movement
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostergard_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ostergard-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The majority of the population of Scandinavia are descended from several North Germanic tribes who originally inhabited the southern part of Scandinavia and spoke a Germanic language that evolved into
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">
        Old Norse
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Icelanders" title="Icelanders">
        Icelanders
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faroese_people" title="Faroese people">
        Faroese
       </a>
       are to a significant extent descended from the Norse and are therefore often seen as Scandinavian. Finland is mainly populated by
       <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">
        Finns
       </a>
       , with a minority of approximately 5%
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish-speaking_population_of_Finland" title="Swedish-speaking population of Finland">
        Swedish speakers
       </a>
       . A small minority of
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">
        Sami people
       </a>
       live in the extreme north of Scandinavia. The
       <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
        Danish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">
        Norwegian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">
        Swedish languages
       </a>
       form a
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">
        dialect continuum
       </a>
       and are known as the Scandinavian languages—all of which are considered
       <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">
        mutually intelligible
       </a>
       with one another.
       <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">
        Faroese
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       , sometimes referred to as insular Scandinavian languages, are intelligible in continental Scandinavian languages only to a limited extent.
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Me%C3%A4nkieli" title="Meänkieli">
        Meänkieli
       </a>
       are closely related to each other and more distantly to the
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" title="Sami languages">
        Sami languages
       </a>
       , but are entirely unrelated to the Scandinavian languages. Apart from these,
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Romani_language" title="Romani language">
        Romani
       </a>
       are recognized minority languages in parts of Scandinavia.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology_and_use">
        <span id="terminology">
        </span>
        Terminology and use
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Terminology and use">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scandinavia_M2002074_lrg.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8800" data-file-width="6800" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Scandinavia_M2002074_lrg.jpg/220px-Scandinavia_M2002074_lrg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Scandinavia_M2002074_lrg.jpg/330px-Scandinavia_M2002074_lrg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Scandinavia_M2002074_lrg.jpg/440px-Scandinavia_M2002074_lrg.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scandinavia_M2002074_lrg.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Satellite photo of the
         <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Peninsula" title="Scandinavian Peninsula">
          Scandinavian Peninsula
         </a>
         , March 2002
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       "Scandinavia" refers to Denmark, Norway and Sweden.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some sources argue for the inclusion of the Faroe Islands, Finland and Iceland,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eb_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-eb-5">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       though that broader region is usually known by the countries concerned as
       <i>
        Norden
       </i>
       (Finnish:
       <i>
        Pohjoismaat
       </i>
       , Icelandic:
       <i>
        Norðurlöndin
       </i>
       , Faroese:
       <i>
        Norðurlond
       </i>
       ), or the
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">
        Nordic countries
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nordic_Council_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nordic_Council-4">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Original_meaning_of_Scandinavia.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="294" data-file-width="376" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Original_meaning_of_Scandinavia.svg/220px-Original_meaning_of_Scandinavia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Original_meaning_of_Scandinavia.svg/330px-Original_meaning_of_Scandinavia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Original_meaning_of_Scandinavia.svg/440px-Original_meaning_of_Scandinavia.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Original_meaning_of_Scandinavia.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Scandinavia originally referred vaguely to
         <a href="/wiki/Scania" title="Scania">
          Scania
         </a>
         , a formerly Danish region that became Swedish in the 17th century
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Scandinavia.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="780" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Map_of_Scandinavia.png/220px-Map_of_Scandinavia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Map_of_Scandinavia.png/330px-Map_of_Scandinavia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Map_of_Scandinavia.png/440px-Map_of_Scandinavia.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Scandinavia.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #aaaaaa; background-color: #ff0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Scandinavia according to the local definition
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #aaaaaa; background-color: #ff8811; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          The extended usage in English which includes Iceland and the Faroe Islands, the
          <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">
           Åland Islands
          </a>
          and Finland.
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The use of Scandinavia as a convenient general term for Denmark, Norway and Sweden is fairly recent. According to some historians, it was adopted and introduced in the eighteenth century, at a time when the ideas about a common heritage started to appear and develop into early literary and linguistic
       <a href="/wiki/Scandinavism" title="Scandinavism">
        Scandinavism
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostergard_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ostergard-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Before this time, the term "Scandinavia" was familiar mainly to classical scholars through
       <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">
        Pliny the Elder
       </a>
       's writings and was used vaguely for Scania and the southern region of the peninsula.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostergard_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ostergard-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As a political term,
       <i>
        Scandinavia
       </i>
       was first used by students agitating for
       <a href="/wiki/Scandinavism" title="Scandinavism">
        pan-Scandinavianism
       </a>
       in the 1830s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostergard_6-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ostergard-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The popular usage of the term in Sweden, Denmark and Norway as a unifying concept became established in the nineteenth century through poems such as
       <a href="/wiki/Hans_Christian_Andersen" title="Hans Christian Andersen">
        Hans Christian Andersen
       </a>
       's "I am a Scandinavian" of 1839. After a visit to Sweden, Andersen became a supporter of early political Scandinavism. In a letter describing the poem to a friend, he wrote: "All at once I understood how related the Swedes, the Danes and the Norwegians are, and with this feeling I wrote the poem immediately after my return: 'We are one people, we are called Scandinavians!'".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The clearest example of the use of
       <i>
        Scandinavia
       </i>
       is Finland, based largely on the fact that most of modern-day Finland was part of the Swedish kingdom for hundreds of years, thus to much of the world associating Finland with all of Scandinavia. However, the creation of a Finnish identity is unique in the region in that it was formed in relation to two different imperial models, the Swedish
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the Russian,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-redescriptions_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-redescriptions-14">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as described by the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Jyv%C3%A4skyl%C3%A4" title="University of Jyväskylä">
        University of Jyväskylä
       </a>
       based editorial board of the Finnish journal
       <i>
        Yearbook of Political Thought and Conceptual History
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olwig_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-olwig-17">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Societal_and_tourism_promotional_organizations">
        Societal and tourism promotional organizations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Societal and tourism promotional organizations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Various promotional agencies of the Nordic countries in the United States (such as
       <a href="/wiki/The_American-Scandinavian_Foundation" title="The American-Scandinavian Foundation">
        The American-Scandinavian Foundation
       </a>
       , established in 1910 by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danish_American" title="Danish American">
        Danish American
       </a>
       industrialist Niels Poulsen) serve to promote market and tourism interests in the region. Today, the five Nordic heads of state act as the organization's patrons and according to the official statement by the organization its mission is "to promote the Nordic region as a whole while increasing the visibility of Denmark, Finland, Iceland, Norway and Sweden in New York City and the United States".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The official tourist boards of Scandinavia sometimes cooperate under one umbrella, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Tourist_Board" title="Scandinavian Tourist Board">
        Scandinavian Tourist Board
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The cooperation was introduced for the Asian market in 1986, when the Swedish national tourist board joined the Danish national tourist board to coordinate intergovernmental promotion of the two countries. Norway's government entered one year later. All five Nordic governments participate in the joint promotional efforts in the United States through the Scandinavian Tourist Board of North America.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span id="Use_of_.22Nordic_countries.22_vs._.22Scandinavia.22">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='Use_of_"Nordic_countries"_vs._"Scandinavia"'>
        Use of "Nordic countries" vs. "Scandinavia"
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title='Edit section: Use of "Nordic countries" vs. "Scandinavia"'>
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For more details on this terminology, see
       <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">
        Nordic countries
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       While the term "Scandinavia" is commonly used for Denmark, Norway and Sweden, the term "Nordic countries" is used unambiguously for Denmark, Norway, Sweden, Finland and Iceland, including their associated territories (
       <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">
        Greenland
       </a>
       , the Faroe Islands and the Åland Islands).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saetre_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-saetre-21">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Scandinavia can thus be considered a subset of the Nordic countries. Furthermore, the term
       <a href="/wiki/Fennoscandia" title="Fennoscandia">
        Fennoscandia
       </a>
       refers to Scandinavia, Finland and
       <a href="/wiki/Karelia" title="Karelia">
        Karelia
       </a>
       , excluding Denmark and overseas territories, but the usage of this term is restricted to
       <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
        geology
       </a>
       when speaking of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fennoscandian_Shield" title="Fennoscandian Shield">
        Fennoscandian Shield
       </a>
       (Baltic Shield).
      </p>
      <p>
       In addition to the mainland Scandinavian countries of:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="740" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Flag_of_Denmark.svg/20px-Flag_of_Denmark.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Flag_of_Denmark.svg/31px-Flag_of_Denmark.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Flag_of_Denmark.svg/40px-Flag_of_Denmark.svg.png 2x" width="20"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">
         Denmark
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Denmark" title="Monarchy of Denmark">
         constitutional monarchy
        </a>
        with a
        <a href="/wiki/Folketing" title="Folketing">
         parliamentary system
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1100" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Norway.svg/21px-Flag_of_Norway.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Norway.svg/32px-Flag_of_Norway.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Norway.svg/41px-Flag_of_Norway.svg.png 2x" width="21"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">
         Norway
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Norway" title="Monarchy of Norway">
         constitutional monarchy
        </a>
        with a
        <a href="/wiki/Storting" title="Storting">
         parliamentary system
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1600" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Sweden.svg/23px-Flag_of_Sweden.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Sweden.svg/35px-Flag_of_Sweden.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Sweden.svg/46px-Flag_of_Sweden.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">
         Sweden
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Sweden" title="Monarchy of Sweden">
         constitutional monarchy
        </a>
        with a
        <a href="/wiki/Riksdag" title="Riksdag">
         parliamentary system
        </a>
        )
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The Nordic countries also consist of:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1800" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Flag_of_Finland.svg/23px-Flag_of_Finland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Flag_of_Finland.svg/35px-Flag_of_Finland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Flag_of_Finland.svg/46px-Flag_of_Finland.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">
         Finland
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_republic" title="Parliamentary republic">
         parliamentary republic
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="576" data-file-width="800" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Flag_of_Iceland.svg/21px-Flag_of_Iceland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Flag_of_Iceland.svg/32px-Flag_of_Iceland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Flag_of_Iceland.svg/42px-Flag_of_Iceland.svg.png 2x" width="21"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">
         Iceland
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_republic" title="Parliamentary republic">
         parliamentary republic
        </a>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="340" data-file-width="520" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Flag_of_%C3%85land.svg/23px-Flag_of_%C3%85land.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Flag_of_%C3%85land.svg/35px-Flag_of_%C3%85land.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Flag_of_%C3%85land.svg/46px-Flag_of_%C3%85land.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/%C3%85land_Islands" title="Åland Islands">
         Åland Islands
        </a>
        (an autonomous province of Finland since 1920)
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1100" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Flag_of_the_Faroe_Islands.svg/21px-Flag_of_the_Faroe_Islands.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Flag_of_the_Faroe_Islands.svg/32px-Flag_of_the_Faroe_Islands.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Flag_of_the_Faroe_Islands.svg/41px-Flag_of_the_Faroe_Islands.svg.png 2x" width="21"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">
         Faroe Islands
        </a>
        (an
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autonomous" title="Autonomous">
         autonomous
        </a>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_Kingdom_of_Denmark" title="Countries of the Kingdom of Denmark">
         country
        </a>
        within the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danish_Realm" title="Danish Realm">
         Danish Realm
        </a>
        , self-governed since 1948)
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_Greenland.svg/23px-Flag_of_Greenland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_Greenland.svg/35px-Flag_of_Greenland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Flag_of_Greenland.svg/45px-Flag_of_Greenland.svg.png 2x" width="23"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">
         Greenland
        </a>
        (an autonomous country within the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danish_Realm" title="Danish Realm">
         Danish Realm
        </a>
        , self-governed since 1979)
       </li>
       <li>
        <span class="flagicon">
         <img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1100" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Norway.svg/21px-Flag_of_Norway.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Norway.svg/32px-Flag_of_Norway.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Norway.svg/41px-Flag_of_Norway.svg.png 2x" width="21"/>
        </span>
        <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">
         Svalbard
        </a>
        , which is under Norwegian sovereignty, is not considered part of Scandinavia as a cultural-historical region, but as a part of the Kingdom of Norway (since 1925) it is part of the Nordic countries (Norden).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Whereas the term "Scandinavia" is relatively straightforward as traditionally relating to the three kingdoms of Denmark, Norway and Sweden there exists some ambiguity as regards the ethnic aspect of the concept in the modern era. Traditionally, the term refers specifically to the majority peoples of Denmark, Norway and Sweden, their states, their Germanic languages and their culture. In the modern era, the term will often include minority peoples such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">
        Sami
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Me%C3%A4nkieli_dialects" title="Meänkieli dialects">
        Meänkieli
       </a>
       speakers in a political and to some extent cultural sense as they are citizens of Scandinavian countries and speak Scandinavian languages either as their first or second language. However, Scandinavian is still also seen as an ethnic term for the Germanic majority peoples of Scandinavia and as such the inclusion of Sami and Finnish speakers can be seen as controversial within these groups.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nordic_Bronze_Age.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="657" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Nordic_Bronze_Age.png/220px-Nordic_Bronze_Age.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Nordic_Bronze_Age.png/330px-Nordic_Bronze_Age.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Nordic_Bronze_Age.png/440px-Nordic_Bronze_Age.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nordic_Bronze_Age.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The original areas inhabited (during the Bronze Age) by the peoples since known as Scandinavians included what is now Northern Germany (particularly
         <a href="/wiki/Schleswig-Holstein" title="Schleswig-Holstein">
          Schleswig-Holstein
         </a>
         ), all of Denmark, southern Sweden and the southern coast of Norway while namesake
         <a href="/wiki/Scania" title="Scania">
          Scania
         </a>
         found itself in the centre
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Scandinavia and
       <a href="/wiki/Scania" title="Scania">
        Scania
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Skåne
       </i>
       , the southernmost province of Sweden) are thought to go back to the
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">
        proto-Germanic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">
        compound
       </a>
       *
       <i>
        Skaðin-awjō
       </i>
       , which appears later in
       <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
        Old English
       </a>
       as
       <i>
        Scedenig
       </i>
       and in
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">
        Old Norse
       </a>
       as
       <i>
        Skáney
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The earliest identified source for the name Scandinavia is
       <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">
        Pliny the Elder
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">
         Natural History
        </a>
       </i>
       , dated to the first century A.D.
      </p>
      <p>
       Various references to the region can also be found in
       <a href="/wiki/Pytheas" title="Pytheas">
        Pytheas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pomponius_Mela" title="Pomponius Mela">
        Pomponius Mela
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">
        Tacitus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">
        Procopius
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Jordanes" title="Jordanes">
        Jordanes
       </a>
       , usually in the form of
       <a href="/wiki/Scandza" title="Scandza">
        Scandza
       </a>
       . It is believed that the name used by Pliny may be of
       <a href="/wiki/West_Germanic_languages" title="West Germanic languages">
        West Germanic
       </a>
       origin, originally denoting Scania.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to some scholars, the Germanic stem can be reconstructed as *
       <i>
        skaðan-
       </i>
       and meaning "danger" or "damage" (English: "scathing", German:
       <i>
        Schaden
       </i>
       , Dutch:
       <i>
        schade
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helle_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Helle-24">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The second segment of the name has been reconstructed as
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Germanic/awj%C5%8D" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Germanic/awjō">
        *
        <i>
         awjō
        </i>
       </a>
       , meaning "land on the water" or "island". The name Scandinavia would then mean "dangerous island", which is considered to refer to the treacherous sandbanks surrounding Scania.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helle_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Helle-24">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skan%C3%B6r-Falsterbo" title="Skanör-Falsterbo">
        Skanör
       </a>
       in Scania, with its long Falsterbo reef, has the same stem (
       <i>
        skan
       </i>
       ) combined with -
       <i>
        ör
       </i>
       , which means "sandbanks".
      </p>
      <p>
       In the reconstructed Germanic root *
       <i>
        Skaðin-awjō
       </i>
       (the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edh" title="Edh">
        edh
       </a>
       represented in Latin by t or d), the first segment is sometimes considered more uncertain than the second segment. The American Heritage Dictionary
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       derives the second segment from
       <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">
        proto-Indo-European
       </a>
       <i>
        *akwa-
       </i>
       , "water", in the sense of "watery land".
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">
        Old Norse
       </a>
       goddess name
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ska%C3%B0i" title="Skaði">
         Skaði
        </a>
       </i>
       , along with
       <i>
        Sca(n)dinavia
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Skáney
       </i>
       , may be related to
       <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">
        Gothic
       </a>
       <i>
        skadus
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
        Old English
       </a>
       <i>
        sceadu
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">
        Old Saxon
       </a>
       <i>
        scado
       </i>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">
        Old High German
       </a>
       <i>
        scato
       </i>
       (meaning "shadow"). Scholar John McKinnell comments that this etymology suggests that the goddess Skaði may have once been a personification of the geographical region of Scandinavia or associated with the underworld.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCKINNELL63_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MCKINNELL63-26">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Pliny_the_Elder.27s_descriptions">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pliny_the_Elder's_descriptions">
        Pliny the Elder's descriptions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Pliny the Elder's descriptions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">
        Pliny
       </a>
       's descriptions of
       <i>
        Scatinavia
       </i>
       and surrounding areas are not always easy to decipher. Writing in the capacity of a Roman admiral, he introduces the northern region by declaring to his Roman readers that there are 23 islands "
       <i>
        Romanis armis cognitae
       </i>
       " ("known to Roman arms") in this area. According to Pliny, the "
       <i>
        clarissima
       </i>
       " ("most famous") of the region's islands is
       <i>
        Scatinavia
       </i>
       , of unknown size. There live the
       <a href="/wiki/Hilleviones" title="Hilleviones">
        Hilleviones
       </a>
       . The belief that Scandinavia was an island became widespread among classical authors during the first century and dominated descriptions of Scandinavia in classical texts during the centuries that followed.
      </p>
      <p>
       Pliny begins his description of the route to
       <i>
        Scatinavia
       </i>
       by referring to the mountain of Saevo (
       <i>
        mons Saevo ibi
       </i>
       ), the Codanus Bay (
       <i>
        Codanus sinus
       </i>
       ) and the Cimbrian promontory.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The geographical features have been identified in various ways. By some scholars, "Saevo" is thought to be the mountainous
       <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">
        Norwegian
       </a>
       coast at the entrance to
       <a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">
        Skagerrak
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Cimbri" title="Cimbri">
        Cimbrian
       </a>
       peninsula is thought to be
       <a href="/wiki/Skagen" title="Skagen">
        Skagen
       </a>
       , the north tip of
       <a href="/wiki/Jutland" title="Jutland">
        Jutland
       </a>
       , Denmark. As described, Saevo and Scatinavia can also be the same place.
      </p>
      <p>
       Pliny mentions Scandinavia one more time: in Book VIII he says that the animal called
       <i>
        achlis
       </i>
       (given in the accusative,
       <i>
        achlin
       </i>
       , which is not Latin) was born on the island of Scandinavia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The animal grazes, has a big upper lip and some mythical attributes.
      </p>
      <p>
       The name "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scandia" title="Scandia">
        Scandia
       </a>
       ", later used as a synonym for Scandinavia, also appears in Pliny's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">
         Naturalis Historia
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Natural History
       </i>
       ), but is used for a group of Northern European islands which he locates north of
       <a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">
        Britannia
       </a>
       . "Scandia" thus does not appear to be denoting the island Scadinavia in Pliny's text. The idea that "
       <i>
        Scadinavia
       </i>
       " may have been one of the "
       <i>
        Scandiae
       </i>
       " islands was instead introduced by
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       (c. 90 – c. 168 AD), a mathematician, geographer and astrologer of Roman Egypt. He used the name "
       <i>
        Skandia
       </i>
       " for the biggest, most easterly of the three "
       <i>
        Scandiai
       </i>
       " islands, which according to him were all located east of
       <a href="/wiki/Jutland" title="Jutland">
        Jutland
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Helle_24-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Helle-24">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Neither Pliny's nor Ptolemy's lists of Scandinavian tribes include the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suiones" title="Suiones">
        Suiones
       </a>
       mentioned by Tacitus. Some early Swedish scholars of the Swedish
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperborean" title="Hyperborean">
        Hyperborean
       </a>
       school
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and of the 19th-century
       <a href="/wiki/Romantic_nationalism" title="Romantic nationalism">
        romantic nationalism
       </a>
       period proceeded to synthesize the different versions by inserting references to the Suiones, arguing that they must have been referred to in the original texts and obscured over time by spelling mistakes or various alterations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Germanic_reconstruction">
        Germanic reconstruction
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Germanic reconstruction">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Latin names in Pliny's text gave rise to different forms in medieval Germanic texts. In Jordanes' history of the
       <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">
        Goths
       </a>
       (AD 551), the form
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Scandza" title="Scandza">
         Scandza
        </a>
       </i>
       is the name used for their original home, separated by sea from the land of Europe (chapter 1, 4).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Where Jordanes meant to locate this quasi-legendary island is still a hotly debated issue, both in scholarly discussions and in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nationalistic" title="Nationalistic">
        nationalistic
       </a>
       discourse of various European countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The form
       <i>
        Scadinavia
       </i>
       as the original home of the
       <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">
        Langobards
       </a>
       appears in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paulus_Diaconus" title="Paulus Diaconus">
        Paulus Diaconus
       </a>
       '
       <i>
        Historia Langobardorum
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but in other versions of
       <i>
        Historia Langobardorum
       </i>
       appear the forms
       <i>
        Scadan
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Scandanan
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Scadanan
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Scatenauge
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Frankish sources used
       <i>
        Sconaowe
       </i>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelweard_(historian)" title="Æthelweard (historian)">
        Aethelweard
       </a>
       , an Anglo-Saxon historian, used
       <i>
        Scani
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-North_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-North-38">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">
        Beowulf
       </a>
       , the forms
       <i>
        Scedenige
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Scedeland
       </i>
       are used while the
       <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">
        Alfredian
       </a>
       translation of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paulus_Orosius" title="Paulus Orosius">
        Orosius
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Wulfstan_of_Hedeby" title="Wulfstan of Hedeby">
        Wulfstan
       </a>
       's travel accounts used the
       <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">
        Old English
       </a>
       <i>
        Sconeg
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-North_38-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-North-38">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sami_etymology">
        Sami etymology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Sami etymology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:This_is_the_center_of_the_sami_town_Kautokeino.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/This_is_the_center_of_the_sami_town_Kautokeino.jpg/220px-This_is_the_center_of_the_sami_town_Kautokeino.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/This_is_the_center_of_the_sami_town_Kautokeino.jpg/330px-This_is_the_center_of_the_sami_town_Kautokeino.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/This_is_the_center_of_the_sami_town_Kautokeino.jpg/440px-This_is_the_center_of_the_sami_town_Kautokeino.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:This_is_the_center_of_the_sami_town_Kautokeino.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Kautokeino" title="Kautokeino">
          Kautokeino
         </a>
         , the main
         <a href="/wiki/Sami_people" title="Sami people">
          Sami
         </a>
         municipality in
         <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">
          Norway
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The earliest
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" title="Sami languages">
        Sami
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yoik" title="Yoik">
        yoik
       </a>
       texts written down refer to the world as
       <i>
        Skadesi-suolo
       </i>
       (north Sami) and
       <i>
        Skađsuâl
       </i>
       (east Sami), meaning "
       <a href="/wiki/Ska%C3%B0i" title="Skaði">
        Skaði
       </a>
       's island". Svennung considers the Sami name to have been introduced as a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loan_word" title="Loan word">
        loan word
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North Germanic languages
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Svennung_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Svennung-39">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "
       <a href="/wiki/Ska%C3%B0i" title="Skaði">
        Skaði
       </a>
       " is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giant_(mythology)" title="Giant (mythology)">
        giant
       </a>
       stepmother of
       <a href="/wiki/Freyr" title="Freyr">
        Freyr
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Freyja" title="Freyja">
        Freyja
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">
        Norse mythology
       </a>
       . It has been suggested that Skaði to some extent is modeled on a Sami woman. The name for Skade's father
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thjazi" title="Thjazi">
        Thjazi
       </a>
       is known in Sami as
       <i>
        Čáhci
       </i>
       , "the waterman"; and her son with Odin,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saeming" title="Saeming">
        Saeming
       </a>
       , can be interpreted as a descendent of
       <i>
        Saam
       </i>
       the Sami population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mundel_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mundel-40">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Older joik texts give evidence of the old Sami belief about living on an island and state that the wolf is known as
       <i>
        suolu gievra
       </i>
       , meaning "the strong one on the island". The Sami
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Place_name" title="Place name">
        place name
       </a>
       <i>
        Sulliidčielbma
       </i>
       means "the island's threshold" and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Saariselk%C3%A4" title="Saariselkä">
         Suoločielgi
        </a>
       </i>
       means "the island's back".
      </p>
      <p>
       In recent
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Substratum" title="Substratum">
        substrate
       </a>
       studies, Sami linguists have examined the initial cluster sk- in words used in Sami and concluded that sk- is a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonotactic" title="Phonotactic">
        phonotactic
       </a>
       structure of alien origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aikio_2004_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Aikio_2004-42">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_etymologies">
        Other etymologies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other etymologies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Another possibility is that all or part of the segments of the name came from the
       <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">
        Mesolithic
       </a>
       people inhabiting the region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giorgio_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Giorgio-43">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In modernity, Scandinavia is a peninsula, but between approximately 10,300 and 9,500 years ago the southern part of Scandinavia was an island separated from the northern peninsula, with water exiting the
       <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">
        Baltic Sea
       </a>
       through the area where
       <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">
        Stockholm
       </a>
       is now located.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Some
       <a href="/wiki/Basque_language" title="Basque language">
        Basque
       </a>
       scholars have presented the idea that the segment
       <i>
        sk
       </i>
       that appears in
       <i>
        *Skaðinawjō
       </i>
       is connected to the name for the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Euzko&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Euzko (page does not exist)">
        Euzko
       </a>
       peoples, akin to Basques, that populated
       <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">
        Paleolithic
       </a>
       Europe. According to some of these intellects, Scandinavian people share particular
       <a href="/wiki/Genetic_marker" title="Genetic marker">
        genetic markers
       </a>
       with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basque_people" title="Basque people">
        Basque people
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giorgio_43-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Giorgio-43">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galdh%C3%B8piggenFromFannar%C3%A5ki.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="529" data-file-width="800" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Galdh%C3%B8piggenFromFannar%C3%A5ki.jpg/220px-Galdh%C3%B8piggenFromFannar%C3%A5ki.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Galdh%C3%B8piggenFromFannar%C3%A5ki.jpg/330px-Galdh%C3%B8piggenFromFannar%C3%A5ki.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Galdh%C3%B8piggenFromFannar%C3%A5ki.jpg/440px-Galdh%C3%B8piggenFromFannar%C3%A5ki.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galdh%C3%B8piggenFromFannar%C3%A5ki.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Galdh%C3%B8piggen" title="Galdhøpiggen">
          Galdhøpiggen
         </a>
         is the highest point in Scandinavia and is a part of the
         <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Mountains" title="Scandinavian Mountains">
          Scandinavian Mountains
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Denmark" title="Geography of Denmark">
        Geography of Denmark
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Finland" title="Geography of Finland">
        Geography of Finland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Iceland" title="Geography of Iceland">
        Geography of Iceland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Norway" title="Geography of Norway">
        Geography of Norway
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Sweden" title="Geography of Sweden">
        Geography of Sweden
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The geography of Scandinavia is extremely varied. Notable are the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Norwegian_fjords" title="List of Norwegian fjords">
        Norwegian fjords
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Scandinavian_Mountains" title="Scandinavian Mountains">
        Scandinavian Mountains
       </a>
       , the flat, low areas in Denmark and the
       <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">
        archipelagos
       </a>
       of Sweden and Norway. Sweden has many lakes and
       <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">
        moraines
       </a>
       , legacies of the
       <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">
        ice age
       </a>
       , which ended about ten millennia ago.
      </p>
      <p>
       The southern and by far most populous regions of Scandinavia have a
       <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">
        temperate climate
       </a>
       . Scandinavia extends north of the
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">
        Arctic Circle
       </a>
       , but has relatively mild weather for its latitude due to the
       <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">
        Gulf Stream
       </a>
       . Many of the Scandinavian mountains have an
       <a href="/wiki/Alpine_tundra" title="Alpine tundra">
        alpine tundra
       </a>
       climate.
      </p>
      <p>
       The climate varies from north to south and from west to east: a marine west coast climate (
       <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification#GROUP_C:_Temperate/mesothermal_climates" title="Köppen climate classification">
        Cfb
       </a>
       ) typical of western Europe dominates in Denmark, southernmost part of Sweden and along the west coast of Norway reaching north to 65°N, with
       <a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">
        orographic lift
       </a>
       giving more mm/year
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">
        precipitation
       </a>
       (&lt;5000 mm) in some areas in western Norway. The central part – from
       <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">
        Oslo
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">
        Stockholm
       </a>
       – has a
       <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">
        humid continental climate
       </a>
       (Dfb), which gradually gives way to
       <a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">
        subarctic climate
       </a>
       (Dfc) further north and cool marine west coast climate (Cfc) along the northwestern coast. A small area along the northern coast east of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Cape,_Norway" title="North Cape, Norway">
        North Cape
       </a>
       has tundra climate (Et) as a result of a lack of summer warmth. The Scandinavian Mountains block the mild and moist air coming from the southwest, thus northern Sweden and the
       <a href="/wiki/Finnmarksvidda" title="Finnmarksvidda">
        Finnmarksvidda
       </a>
       plateau in Norway receive little precipitation and have cold winters. Large areas in the Scandinavian mountains have
       <a href="/wiki/Alpine_tundra" title="Alpine tundra">
        alpine tundra
       </a>
       climate.
      </p>
      <p>
       The warmest temperature ever recorded in Scandinavia is 38.0 °C in
       <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A5lilla" title="Målilla">
        Målilla
       </a>
       (Sweden).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The coldest temperature ever recorded is −52.6 °C in
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Vuoggatj%C3%A5lme&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Vuoggatjålme (page does not exist)">
        Vuoggatjålme
       </a>
       (Sweden).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smhi.se_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-smhi.se-46">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The coldest month was February 1985 in Vittangi (Sweden) with a mean of −27.2 °C.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smhi.se_46-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-smhi.se-46">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Southwesterly winds further warmed by
       <a href="/wiki/Foehn_wind" title="Foehn wind">
        foehn wind
       </a>
       can give warm temperatures in narrow Norwegian fjords in winter.
       <a href="/wiki/Tafjord" title="Tafjord">
        Tafjord
       </a>
       has recorded 17.9 °C in January and
       <a href="/wiki/Sunndal" title="Sunndal">
        Sunndal
       </a>
       18.9 °C in February.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Languages_in_Scandinavia">
        Languages in Scandinavia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Languages in Scandinavia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North Germanic languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" title="Sami languages">
        Sami languages
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Finnic_languages" title="Finnic languages">
        Finnic languages
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scandoromani" title="Scandoromani">
        Scandoromani
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Two language groups have coexisted on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scandinavian_peninsula" title="Scandinavian peninsula">
        Scandinavian peninsula
       </a>
       since prehistory—the
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North Germanic languages
       </a>
       (Scandinavian languages) and the
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" title="Sami languages">
        Sami languages
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Due to later migrations,
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Romani_language" title="Romani language">
        Romani
       </a>
       have also been spoken for over a hundred years. Denmark also has a minority of
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       -speakers. More recent migrations has added even more languages. Apart from Sami and the languages of minority groups speaking a variant of the majority language of a neighboring state, the following minority languages in Scandinavia are protected under the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">
        European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Yiddish" title="Yiddish">
        Yiddish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Romani_language" title="Romani language">
        Romani Chib, Romanes
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scandoromani" title="Scandoromani">
        Romani
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="North_Germanic_languages">
        North Germanic languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: North Germanic languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/North_Germanic_languages" title="North Germanic languages">
        North Germanic languages
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nordiska_spr%C3%A5k.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="552" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Nordiska_spr%C3%A5k.PNG/220px-Nordiska_spr%C3%A5k.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Nordiska_spr%C3%A5k.PNG/330px-Nordiska_spr%C3%A5k.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Nordiska_spr%C3%A5k.PNG/440px-Nordiska_spr%C3%A5k.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nordiska_spr%C3%A5k.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Continental Scandinavian languages:
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #6262ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Danish
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Norwegian
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00009f; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Swedish
         </div>
         Insular Scandinavian languages:
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00ffff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Faroese
         </div>
         <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column">
          <span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00ff00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">
          </span>
          Icelandic
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The North Germanic languages of Scandinavia are traditionally divided into an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_Scandinavian" title="East Scandinavian">
        East Scandinavian
       </a>
       branch (
       <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
        Danish
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">
        Swedish
       </a>
       ) and a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Scandinavian" title="West Scandinavian">
        West Scandinavian
       </a>
       branch (
       <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">
        Norwegian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">
        Faroese
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but because of changes appearing in the languages since 1600 the East Scandinavian and West Scandinavian branches are now usually reconfigured into Insular Scandinavian (
       <i>
        ö-nordisk
       </i>
       /
       <i>
        øy-nordisk
       </i>
       ) featuring
       <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">
        Icelandic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">
        Faroese
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Continental Scandinavian (
       <i>
        Skandinavisk
       </i>
       ), comprising Danish, Norwegian and Swedish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heine_2006_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Heine_2006-51">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The modern division is based on the degree of mutual comprehensibility between the languages in the two branches.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torp_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Torp-52">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The populations of the Scandinavian countries, with common Scandinavian roots in language, can—at least with some training—understand each other's
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_language" title="Standard language">
        standard languages
       </a>
       as they appear in print and are heard on radio and television.
      </p>
      <p>
       The reason Danish, Swedish and the two official written versions of Norwegian (
       <i>
        Nynorsk
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Bokmål
       </i>
       ) are traditionally viewed as different languages, rather than dialects of one common language, is that each is a well-established standard language in its respective country. Danish, Swedish and Norwegian have since medieval times been influenced to varying degrees by
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German" title="Middle Low German">
        Middle Low German
       </a>
       and standard German. A substantial amount of that influence was a by-product of the economic activity generated by the
       <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">
        Hanseatic League
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Norwegians are accustomed to variation and may perceive Danish and Swedish only as slightly more distant dialects. This is because they have two official written standards, in addition to the habit of strongly holding on to local dialects. The people of
       <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">
        Stockholm
       </a>
       , Sweden and
       <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">
        Copenhagen
       </a>
       , Denmark have the greatest difficulty in understanding other Scandinavian languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the Faroe Islands and Iceland, learning
       <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">
        Danish
       </a>
       is mandatory. This causes Faroese people as well as Icelandic people to become bilingual in two very distinct North Germanic languages, making it relatively easy for them to understand the other two Mainland Scandinavian languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although Iceland was under the political control of Denmark until a much later date (1918), very little influence and borrowing from Danish has occurred in the Icelandic language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Icelandic remained the preferred language among the ruling classes in Iceland. Danish was not used for official communications, most of the royal officials were of Icelandic descent and the language of the church and law courts remained Icelandic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Finnish">
        Finnish
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Finnish">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The Scandinavian languages are (as a language family) entirely unrelated to
       <a href="/wiki/Finnish_language" title="Finnish language">
        Finnish
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Estonian_language" title="Estonian language">
        Estonian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sami_languages" title="Sami languages">
        Sami languages
       </a>
       , which as
       <a href="/wiki/Uralic_languages" title="Uralic languages">
        Uralic languages
       </a>
       are distantly related to
       <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">
        Hungarian
       </a>
       . Owing to the close proximity, there is still a great deal of borrowing from the Swedish and Norwegian languages in the Finnish and Sami languages.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sapmi_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sapmi-58">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The long history of linguistic influence of Swedish on Finnish is also due to the fact that Finnish, the language of the majority in Finland, was treated as a minority language while Finland was part of Sweden. Finnish-speakers had to learn Swedish in order to advance to higher positions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Swedish spoken in today's Finland includes a lot of words that are borrowed from Finnish, whereas the written language remains closer to that of Sweden.
      </p>
      <p>
       Finland is officially bilingual, with Finnish and Swedish having mostly the same status at national level. Finland's majority population are
       <a href="/wiki/Finns" title="Finns">
        Finns
       </a>
       , whose mother tongue is either Finnish (approximately 95%), Swedish or both. The Swedish speaking minority lives mainly on the coast from the city of Porvoo, in the Gulf of Finland, to the city of Kokkola, up in the Bothnian Bay. The Åland Islands, an autonomous province of Finland, situated in the Baltic Sea between Finland and Sweden, is entirely Swedish speaking. Children are taught the other official language at school and for Swedish-speakers this is Finnish (usually from the 3rd grade) while for Finnish-speakers it is Swedish (usually from the 3rd, 5th or 7th grade). [someone please edit the above two paragraphs, as they are clearly lifted off some 1970's textbook, and contain numerous inaccuracies and falsehoods]
      </p>
      <p>
       Finnish speakers constitute a
       <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">
        language minority
       </a>
       in Sweden and Norway. There are also languages derived from Finnish, having evolved separately, known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Me%C3%A4nkieli" title="Meänkieli">
        Meänkieli
       </a>
       in Sweden and
       <a href="/wiki/Kven_language" title="Kven language">
        Kven
       </a>
       in Norway.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sami_languages">
        Sami languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Scandinavia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Sami languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sami_languages_large_2.png">
